            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="d197b86f658d459c93777c6f083d8b83">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '3c947678-88aa-48c9-afbf-df9f6a560368'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

                     <p>Institute of Fuel Cells, School of Mechanical Engineering, Shanghai Jiao Tong University, Shanghai, 200240 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Shen/Shuiyun">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Shadike/Zulipiya" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a9" data-db-target-for="a9" aria-controls="a9" aria-haspopup="true" id="a9_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Zulipiya Shadike<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a9" aria-labelledby="a9_Ctrl" role="region" id="a9">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d167077223" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Enhancing the kinetics of lattice oxygen redox (LOR) in P2-type layered sodium oxide cathodes is crucial for the advancement of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with superior energy and power densities. Electronic structure regulation stands out as a highly effective approach to address the inherent limitations of P2-type layered oxides with LOR, including sluggish kinetics, phase transitions, voltage hysteresis, and local structural distortion. Herein, a strategy involving the introduction of CoO<sub>6</sub> octahedra with <i>π</i>-interaction into Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Fe<sub>0.3−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Co<i><sub>x</sub></i>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.15, 0.3) cathodes to facilitate Na-ion transport is proposed. Furthermore, the impact of FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedra with <i>σ</i>-interaction in P2-type cathodes on electrochemical performance is comprehensively investigated. Through multimodal in-situ and ex-situ characterization techniques, it is revealed that Co─O with <i>π</i>-interaction effectively mitigates P2-OP4 phase transitions by strengthening Na─O, reduces voltage hysteresis, and stabilizes the local structure. Consequently, Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Co<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> demonstrates enhanced Na-ion diffusion kinetics, leading to improved rate performance and a reversible capacity of 55 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C, significantly outperforming cathodes with Fe─O <i>σ</i>-interaction. Moreover, when coupled with hard carbon, the full cell achieves a remarkable energy density of 395&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (on cathode) at 0.1 C, with a capacity retention of 75% over 100 cycles at 1 C.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202409732-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202409732-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Energy storage systems (ESSs) with exceptional power density is of great significance to satisfy the demand of fast-paced lifestyle in modern society.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0001_R_d167077066e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nat. Energy.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a branch of ESSs, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) exhibit fertile potential due to the low cost, abundant Na resources and good safety, especially in large-scale energy storage fields, electric vehicles, and portable devices.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0002_R_d167077066e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater. eScience.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> High energy density and fast-charging capability poses the great challenge to the ion transportations and kinetics of electrochemical reactions in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0003_R_d167077066e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Over the past two decades, cathodes for SIBs have undergone significant development, categorized into transition metal oxides, Prussian blue and its analogues, polyanionic compounds, among others.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0004_R_d167077066e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Storage Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Prussian blue cathodes are promising due to their high theoretical specific capacity,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0005_R_d167077066e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nano Lett.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> while polyanionic compounds are fascinating for their NASICON structure with robust crystallinity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0006_R_d167077066e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ACS Nano.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, P2-type sodium oxide cathodes have been attached much attention for high Na-ion conductivity and good structural integrity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0007_R_d167077066e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Science. eScience.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Especially, extending the state of charge to achieve oxygen redox makes P2-type sodium oxides boost higher energy density, which is the focus in the investigations of SIBs cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0008_R_d167077066e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nat. Chem.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the bulk oxygen redox in P2-type sodium oxides suffers sluggish kinetics,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0009_R_d167077066e584" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Commun.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> along with the phase transitions of P2-O2<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0010_R_d167077066e589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nature.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> or P2-OP4,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0011_R_d167077066e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Energy.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> serious voltage hysteresis<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0012_R_d167077066e600" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Sustain.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and degeneration of local structure,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0013_R_d167077066e605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Sustainability.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> leading to the inferior rate performance and power density of the cathodes. Hence, inhibiting phase transition, suppressing voltage hysteresis and stabilizing the local structure are the effective strategies to promote the kinetics of bulk oxygen redox chemistry in SIBs cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0014_R_d167077066e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> More specifically, the phase transition of P2-O2 or P2-OP4 is essentially the transformation from NaO<sub>6</sub> prism to NaO<sub>6</sub> octahedron in layered oxides, showing as the change of Na<i>─</i>O bonding.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0015_R_d167077066e622" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Environ. Sci.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the robust Na<i>─</i>O bonding is crucial to inhibit the formation octahedral NaO<sub>6</sub> and phase transitions even during oxygen redox process under high potential in P2-type sodium oxides, further improving the kinetics of Na-ion intercalation/deintercalation.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we demonstrate the effectiveness of Fe─O and Co─O octahedrons with <i>σ</i> and <i>π</i>-interactions, respectively, on the structure stability and rate performance by designing three P2-type sodium oxides cathodes noted as Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Co<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, and Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. The correlation between phase transitions, oxygen redox kinetics, and oxygen local structure are systematically investigated. In-situ X-ray diffractions (XRD), X-ray adsorption spectroscopies (XAS), and density function theoretical (DFT) calculations reveal that the CoO<sub>6</sub> octahedron with <i>π</i>-interaction effectively suppresses P2-OP4 phase transition and strengthens oxygen local structure during the charge/discharge process. Benefiting from the robust Na<i>─</i>O bonding induced by Co─O <i>π</i>-interaction, Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Co<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> exhibits the best rate performance (55 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C) and fast kinetics among the three samples. Moreover, the full cell with hard carbon anode and Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Co<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode delivers a high energy density of 395&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C (on cathode) and the retention of 75% after 100 cycles (at 1 C).</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202409732-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202409732-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202409732-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202409732-sec-0030-title">2.1 Analysis of <i>σ/π-</i>Interactions in TMO<sub>6</sub> Octahedron</h3>
            
            <p>Previous investigations have revealed that the 3<i>d</i>-orbital of transition metal (TM) element is splitting in the octahedral coordination, forming <i>t</i><sub>2g</sub> and <i>e</i><sub>g</sub> orbitals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0016_R_d167077066e734" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Energy.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this case, as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409732-fig-0001">1a,b</a>, <i>t</i><sub>2g</sub><sup>3</sup><i>e</i><sub>g</sub><sup>2</sup> of Fe<sup>3+</sup> displays as a high spin state in FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedron, while <i>t</i><sub>2g</sub><sup>6</sup><i>e</i><sub>g</sub><sup>0</sup> of Co<sup>3+</sup> displays as a low spin state in CoO<sub>6</sub> octahedron.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0017_R_d167077066e770" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nat. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high spin state of Fe<sup>3+</sup> results in the partial contribution of <i>σ</i>-interaction in Fe<i>─</i>O bonding due to the hybridization between <i>e</i><sub>g</sub> of Fe 3<i>d</i> and 2<i>p</i> (<i>σ</i>) of O, indicating the mixture of strong <i>σ</i>-interaction and weak <i>π</i>-interaction in FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedron. As for the low spin state of Co<sup>3+</sup>, the bonding electrons totally come from Co <i>t</i><sub>2g</sub> and O 2<i>p</i>(<i>π</i>), resulting in the total contribution of weak <i>π</i>-interaction in Co<i>─</i>O bonding in CoO<sub>6</sub> octahedron. To explore the effect of Fe─O <i>σ</i> and Co─O <i>π</i>-interactions on oxygen redox chemistry, we designed a Co concentration gradient in three P2-type cathodes of P2-Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (denoted as Fe<sub>0.3</sub>), P2-Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (denoted as Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>), and P2-Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Co<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (denoted as Co<sub>0.3</sub>). The three samples were synthesized via solid-state sintering process under the same conditions. Powder X-ray diffraction patterns (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409732-fig-0001">1d,e,f</a>) illustrate the typical layered crystal structures without impurities in the three samples, indicating the Co and Fe elements were successfully introduced into the TM layers. The Rietveld refinements indicated that as-synthesized samples all belonged to the <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> symmetry. Moreover, the demonstration of expected Li/Fe/Co ratios was obtained by the inductively coupled plasma atomic emission spectrometry (ICP-AES, Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). The micro areas of the prepared samples were detected by the transmission electron microscope and the energy dispersive X-ray (EDX) spectroscopy indicated the uniform distributions of Fe, Co, Mn in three samples (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). These characterization results confirmed the successful synthesis of the designed samples as expected. To verify the spin state of Fe in our prepared samples (Fe<sub>0.3</sub> and Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>), soft X-ray adsorption spectroscopies (XAS) of Fe <i>L</i>-edge were performed on pristine Fe<sub>0.3</sub> and Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409732-fig-0001">1c</a>). Two peaks of Fe <i>L</i><sub>3</sub>-edge at 706.8, 708.8&nbsp;eV and another two peaks of Fe <i>L</i><sub>2</sub>-edge located at 720.0, 722.0&nbsp;eV can be observed from Fe <i>L</i>-edge partial fluorescence yield (PFY) XAS spectra,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0018_R_d167077066e905" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. J. Phys. Chem. Lett.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> demonstrating the high spin state of Fe<sup>3+</sup> in pristine Fe<sub>0.3</sub> and Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub> without any contribution of low spin state of Fe<sup>3+</sup>, further confirming the <i>σ</i>-interaction contribution of Fe─O in the pristine Fe<sub>0.3</sub> and Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202409732-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c685ec12-908d-4b66-9ea8-1a9a8bd04348/adfm202409732-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c685ec12-908d-4b66-9ea8-1a9a8bd04348/adfm202409732-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8654a5b8-5051-4c46-8183-b03dacbdab70/adfm202409732-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c685ec12-908d-4b66-9ea8-1a9a8bd04348/adfm202409732-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202409732-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202409732" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Diagrams of the band structures of a) FeO<sub>6</sub> and b) CoO<sub>6</sub> octahedron based on molecular orbital diagrams of <i>O</i><sub>h</sub> symmetry. c) PFY Fe <i>L</i>-edge XAS spectra of Fe<sub>0.3</sub> and Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub> at pristine state. XRD patterns of d) Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, e) Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, f) Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Co<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, and the corresponding Rietveld refinement fitting results.</div>

         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202409732-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202409732-sec-0040-title">2.2 Performance Promotion by Co─O <i>π</i>-Interaction</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical performance of the three prepared samples was compared using Na-half coin cells. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves exhibit distinct oxidation peaks above 4.0&nbsp;V, corresponding to oxygen oxidation (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). However, the three cathodes display varying positions of the oxidation peak, with Fe<sub>0.3</sub> exhibiting the lowest oxidation potential, indicating easier oxidation of oxygen in Fe<sub>0.3</sub> compared to the other two cathodes. During the discharge process, noticeable reduction peaks are observed in Co<sub>0.3</sub>, Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>, and two peaks in Fe<sub>0.3</sub>. Specifically, in Co<sub>0.3</sub>, the Mn<sup>4+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup>, Co<sup>4+</sup>/Co<sup>3+</sup>, and O redox processes occur at 1.60, 3.60, and 4.2&nbsp;V, respectively. In Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>, the redox peaks are observed at 1.70, 3.50, and 3.80&nbsp;V. For Fe<sub>0.3</sub>, the Mn<sup>4+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> and O redox processes occur at 1.80 and 3.0&nbsp;V. These redox peaks indicate that Fe does not contribute significantly to the capacity, whereas Co does. For the subsequent two cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), Co<sub>0.3</sub> shows good reversibility in oxygen oxidation peaks (beyond 4.0&nbsp;V). In contrast, noticeable decreases in these peaks are observed in Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub> and Fe<sub>0.3</sub> upon iron substitution in the cathodes, corresponding to the different voltage hysteresis observed in these samples. During the discharge process, the oxygen reduction peak (beyond 4.0&nbsp;V) gradually merges with the Co reduction peak, as clearly seen in both Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>a,&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3b</a> (Supporting Information). This phenomenon appears unique to cobalt substitution, as evidenced by the consistent oxygen reduction peak in the initial three cycles. The different O redox potential values among the three cathodes reflect varying voltage hysteresis, with the Co composition demonstrating the ability to suppress voltage hysteresis. The charge curves of all three samples at 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (0.2 C) display the slope shapes before 4.0&nbsp;V and the obvious plateaus above 4.0&nbsp;V (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409732-fig-0002">2a</a>). The curves are different from P2-NaLiMnO<sub>2</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0010_R_d167077066e1076" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nature.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> or P2-NaLiTiMnO<sub>2</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0019_R_d167077066e1083" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nat. Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with oxygen redox plateau only, thus it can be deduced that Fe and Co participated in charge compensation as well. The different voltage plateaus above 4.0&nbsp;V indicate that abilities to lose electrons of oxygen atoms in three samples are different. More specifically, oxidation of oxygen atom in Fe<sub>0.3</sub> is easier than that of in Co<sub>0.3</sub>, which is attributed to the half-filled <i>e</i><sub>g</sub> and <i>t</i><sub>2g</sub> orbital in Fe<sub>0.3</sub>, easier losing electrons than the filled <i>t</i><sub>2g</sub> orbital in Co<sub>0.3</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409732-fig-0001">1a,b</a>). In the discharge process, Fe<sub>0.3</sub> exhibited the largest voltage hysteresis (≈700&nbsp;mV), while Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub> and Co<sub>0.3</sub> displayed as ≈500 and ≈200&nbsp;mV, indicating the Co-substitution can effectively alleviate the voltage hysteresis. Moreover, the average discharge voltage and charge/discharge curves of Co<sub>0.3</sub> demonstrates the slowest rate of decrease over cycles (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). Co<sub>0.3</sub> exhibits a voltage hysteresis of 209&nbsp;mV over 50 cycles, much lower than Fe<sub>0.3</sub> (499&nbsp;mV over 50 cycles) and Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub> (465&nbsp;mV over 50 cycles). This enhanced stability may be attributed to the delocalized electrons resulting from <i>π</i>-interactions within the CoO<sub>6</sub> octahedron. The rate capabilities of three samples were evaluated as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409732-fig-0002">2b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information), which clearly demonstrates that Co<sub>0.3</sub> shows best rate performance, while Fe<sub>0.3</sub> possess worse rate capability. Even at higher current density of 10 C, Co<sub>0.3</sub> and Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub> exhibited the discharge capacities of 50 and 40 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, indicating the Co-substitution can largely promote the rate performance. The electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) tests illustrate that the improved rate capability of Co<sub>0.3</sub> is attributed to the reduced charge transfer impedance and increased conductivity (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). Regarding the cycling stability of Co<sub>0.3</sub> under fast charging conditions, a high capacity retention of 77.5% was achieved over 200 cycles at 5 C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). This improvement could be resulted from the alleviative voltage hysteresis of CoO<sub>6</sub> octahedron, demonstrating that Co improves the oxygen redox kinetics. Furthermore, the full cells were assembled by using Co<sub>0.3</sub> as cathode and pre-sodiated hard carbon (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information) as anode to evaluate the practical application of as-synthesized sample with oxygen redox. A discharge capacity of 145 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a high energy density of 395&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (calculated based on cathode) were obtained in the full cell at 0.1 C within the voltage range of 1.3–4.5&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409732-fig-0002">2c</a>). The cycling stability of hard carbon || Co<sub>0.3</sub> full cell was evaluated at 1 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409732-fig-0002">2d</a>), which exhibited a reversible capacity of 127 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with the high capacity retention of 75% for 100 cycles and showed better rate capability than other several full cells with P2-layered oxide cathodes (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202409732-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/86e633ce-ddbc-46ec-b4aa-a4aca38ac030/adfm202409732-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/86e633ce-ddbc-46ec-b4aa-a4aca38ac030/adfm202409732-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6e16e24e-b513-49f4-9727-b32ef78aeecc/adfm202409732-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/86e633ce-ddbc-46ec-b4aa-a4aca38ac030/adfm202409732-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202409732-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202409732" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Charge/discharge curves of three prepared cathodes in Na half cells at 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (0.2 C). b) Rate capabilities of three cathodes in Na half cells. c) Charge/discharge curves of hard carbon || Co<sub>0.3</sub> sodium-ion full cell at 0.1 C. Inset is the schematic diagram of sodium-ion full cell. d) Cycling capability of hard carbon || Co<sub>0.3</sub> sodium-ion full cell at 1&nbsp;&nbsp;C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202409732-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202409732-sec-0050-title">2.3 Mechanisms of Charge Compensation and Local Structure Strengthening</h3>
            
            <p>The charge compensation mechanisms among Fe, Co, Mn, and O were analyzed through the XAS spectra of these elements at various states of charges (SOCs) combined with partial density of states (pDOS) calculations. The structure models of the three pristine samples are displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information). The corresponding pDOS results are presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information), while the energy level diagrams were simplified, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information). According to the relationship between Hubbard bands (<i>U</i>) and charge transfer term (Δ), a result of Δ≈<i>U</i>/2 was obtained in all three samples, suggesting the oxygen redox chemistry is reversible in the three samples.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0016_R_d167077066e1245" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Energy.">16</a>, <a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0020_R_d167077066e1245" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The X-ray absorption near edge structure (XANES) spectra at Mn <i>K</i>-edge of three samples show the obvious energy shifts at various SOCs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information), demonstrating the valence change between Mn<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup>, which is consisting with the previous reports.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0014_R_d167077066e1260" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The discharged state of the Mn <i>K-</i>edge exhibits higher energy compared to the LiMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> standard sample, indicating partial reduction of Mn<sup>4+</sup> to Mn<sup>3+</sup> during the discharge process. This reduction helps mitigate the Jahn–Teller effect of Mn<sup>3+</sup> to some extent and prevents distortion of the MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedron, consistent with minimal variations observed in the first-shell Mn─O distances (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>In XANES spectra at Fe <i>K</i>-edge of Fe<sub>0.3</sub> (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409732-fig-0003">3a</a>), there is nearly no obvious energy shift at 0.5-height intensity at various SOCs, indicating the very little capacity contribution of Fe in the charge/discharge process. However, the white line of Fe <i>K</i>-edge exhibited the obvious shift from 7125.9&nbsp;eV (at pristine and discharged states) to 7126.9&nbsp;eV (at charged states). In addition, the pre-edge of Fe <i>K</i>-edge shows stronger peak at charged states and disappears at the discharged state. Such variations of Fe <i>K</i>-edge XAS indicated the obvious local structure changes in Fe environment, which could be associated with the influence of <i>σ</i>-interaction in Fe─O. The extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) spectra of Fe also demonstrated the local structural variation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409732-fig-0003">3b</a>). It can be observed that the distance of Fe─O shrinks at charged states and extends back at discharged state with no obvious change of Fe─TM distance. The variation of Fe─O distance at different SOCs also reflects the distortion of FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedron, which is contributed to the variation of white line and pre-edge of Fe <i>K</i>-edge. As for Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub> at different SOCs, the variations of XANES spectra at Fe <i>K</i>-edge were similar as the evolution of Fe <i>K</i>-edge XAS in Fe<sub>0.3</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15a</a>, Supporting Information). The Fourier transformed EXAFS spectra illustrate the smaller variations of Fe─O than Fe<sub>0.3</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409732-fig-0003">3d</a>), indicating that the Co-substitution alleviates the average distortion of FeO<sub>6</sub> local structure. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409732-fig-0003">3c</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a</a> (Supporting Information), the XANES spectra at Co <i>K</i>-edge of Co<sub>0.3</sub> and Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub> displayed energy shifts of Co <i>K</i>-edge, indicating the Co valence state variation and capacity contribution from Co during the charge/discharge process. Benefitting from the <i>π</i>-interaction of Co─O, the distance of Co─O generates the slight variation at different SOCs as shown in Co-EXAFS spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16b,c</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202409732-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6c82947c-2c75-4b4f-9ef9-619a07527a4a/adfm202409732-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6c82947c-2c75-4b4f-9ef9-619a07527a4a/adfm202409732-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a20dc7b7-b26a-438b-8f02-cb66e7c31510/adfm202409732-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6c82947c-2c75-4b4f-9ef9-619a07527a4a/adfm202409732-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202409732-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202409732" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Fe <i>K</i>-edge XAS of Fe<sub>0.3</sub> collected at different SOCs in the initial two cycles. b) The Fourier transformed EXAFS spectra extracted from hard XAS spectra of Fe <i>K</i>-edge at different SOCs in Fe<sub>0.3</sub>. c) Co <i>K</i>-edge XAS of Co<sub>0.3</sub> collected at different SOCs in the initial two cycles. d) The Fourier transformed EXAFS spectra extracted from hard XAS spectra of Fe <i>K</i>-edge at different SOCs in Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>. TEY O <i>K</i>-edge XAS of e) Fe<sub>0.3</sub> and f) Co<sub>0.3</sub> at different SOCs.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>O <i>K</i>-edge soft XAS of the three samples were obtained at different SOCs to confirm the capacity contribution from oxygen redox (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409732-fig-0003">3e,f</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15b</a>, Supporting Information). During the charge process, the intensity of <i>e</i><sub>g</sub> (≈532.8&nbsp;eV) orbital increases, indicating the increasing composition of unoccupied orbitals at charged states and demonstrating the loss of electrons in O 2<i>p</i>−TM 3<i>d</i> hybridized orbitals. In discharge process, the intensity of <i>e</i><sub>g</sub> and <i>t</i><sub>2g</sub> (≈530.7&nbsp;eV) deceases, assigned to the transitions to occupied O 2<i>p</i>-TM 3<i>d</i> hybridized orbitals. The results demonstrated the reversible oxygen redox occurred in all the three cathodes.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202409732-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202409732-sec-0060-title">2.4 Effects of <i>π</i>-Interaction on Phase Transition and Diffusion Kinetics</h3>
            
            <p>To explore the effects of Co─O <i>π</i>-interaction on phase transitions and Na-ion diffusion kinetics during Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation, in-situ XRD and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) tests were performed and compared among the three samples. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409732-fig-0004">4a</a> for Fe<sub>0.3</sub>, during the charge process below 4.0&nbsp;V, the (002) peak shifts to the low angle and then slightly to the high angle, which can be attributed to the repulsive force of oxygen-layer and the pillar effect of Na<sup>+</sup> to the layered structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0021_R_d167077066e1457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Above 4.0&nbsp;V, where the oxygen redox is triggered, an obvious peak at 16.8° generates, corresponding to the phase transition from P2 to OP4. In the subsequent discharge process, the OP4 peak gradually disappears and P2 phase reappears. In Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>, although the P2-OP4 phase transition can also be observed, the intensity of OP4 peak is much lower than that of Fe<sub>0.3</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409732-fig-0004">4b</a>). Interestingly, there is no OP4 phase being detected during the charge/discharge process in Co<sub>0.3</sub>. In the in situ XRD pattern of Co<sub>0.3</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409732-fig-0004">4c</a>), the strong and continuous P2 peak was observed without any other new peak generating during the whole process, indicating a solid-solution process with single P2 phase in Co<sub>0.3</sub>. The in-situ XRD results revel that Co─O <i>π</i>-interaction effectively suppresses the P2-OP4 phase transition, while a serious distortion of FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedron during charging is demonstrated as the direct cause of inducing P2-OP4 phase transition.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202409732-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/be5b407a-c547-4112-a065-bd0c83468346/adfm202409732-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/be5b407a-c547-4112-a065-bd0c83468346/adfm202409732-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/78108b1d-061b-4268-b4fe-caf2aad2e4b6/adfm202409732-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/be5b407a-c547-4112-a065-bd0c83468346/adfm202409732-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202409732-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202409732" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In-situ XRD patterns of a) Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, b) Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, and c) Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Co<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> during the initial two cycles within 1.5–4.5&nbsp;V at the current density of 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. d) The evaluation of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusivities of the three cathodes during the discharge process in the first cycle. The integrating of Na─O covalence at different SOCs in e) O─(NaLiMnCo) and f) O─(NaLiMnFe) local structures existing in three samples based on the crystal orbital Hamilton population (COHP) calculations.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The Na-ion diffusion kinetics of three samples was evaluated by the GITT tests. The shapes of GITT curves in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17a–c</a> (Supporting Information) are very similar to the galvanostatic charge–discharge curves, which exhibit the obvious oxygen redox plateau over 4.0&nbsp;V. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusivities of the three samples were obtained from the GITT curves. In both charge (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17d</a>, Supporting Information) and discharge process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409732-fig-0004">4d</a>), Co<sub>0.3</sub> exhibited the largest diffusivity, followed by Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>, and Fe<sub>0.3</sub> shows the lowest diffusivity. This demonstrates Co-substitution in Fe<sub>0.3</sub> effectively improve the Na-ion diffusion kinetics. The result is also consistent with the difference in rate capability of three samples. By combining with the phase evolutions, it can be deduced that OP4 phase transition is the culprit of the sluggish kinetics in P2 sodium oxides. This can be explained as hard breaking of the octahedral Na─O in OP4.</p>
            
            <p>To reveal influence of Co─O <i>π</i>-interaction on P2-OP4 phase transitions, we utilized DFT calculations to investigate the crystal orbital Hamilton population (COHP) of Na─O in three samples at different SOCs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). The positive values of <i>─</i>COHP are assigned to the bonding states, while the integrated <i>─</i>COHP represents the order of the Na─O covalence.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0022_R_d167077066e1582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The local structure of O─(NaLiMnCo) exists in both Co<sub>0.3</sub> and Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub> crystal structure, where the Na─O covalence increases during the charge process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409732-fig-0004">4e</a>), indicating the Na─O is become stronger with Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation. However, in the local structure of O─(NaLiMnFe) of Fe<sub>0.3</sub>, the Na─O covalence decreases with Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202409732-fig-0004">4f</a>), indicating the weaker Na─O under the influence of Fe. Due to the decreasing of Na─O covalence in Fe<sub>0.3</sub>, the ionic bond composition of Na─O increases, which means that the combination between Na and O is easier to change from NaO<sub>6</sub> prism to NaO<sub>6</sub> octahedron. Thus, the phase transition from P2 to OP4 is easier to occur in Fe<sub>0.3</sub>. Incorporating CoO<sub>6</sub> octahedra with <i>π</i>-interaction into Fe<sub>0.3</sub> allows for the regulation of the oxygen electronic structure within O─(NaLiMnCo), promoting the formation of robust Na<i>─</i>O bonds that suppress the P2-OP4 phase transition. This encourages the generation of more prismatic environments where the energy barrier for Na diffusion is lower compared to octahedral sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0023_R_d167077066e1625" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, through <i>π</i>-interaction regulation, the Na-ion kinetics during the LOR process are enhanced, thereby improving the rate capability.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202409732-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202409732-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we systematically investigated the mechanisms of improving of oxygen redox kinetics by CoO<sub>6</sub> octahedron with <i>π</i>-interaction in P2-type sodium oxides cathodes. This promotion of oxygen redox kinetics was revealed in three aspects: 1) Inhibiting P2-OP4 phase transition. In-situ XRD, GITT, and COHP calculations demonstrates the robust Na─O in O─(NaLiMnCo) can inhibit the transformation from NaO<sub>6</sub> prism to NaO<sub>6</sub> octahedron, inducing larger Na diffusion coefficient. 2) Suppressing voltage hysteresis. The delocalized electrons of <i>π</i>-interaction in CoO<sub>6</sub> octahedron effectively mitigates the large degeneration of electron states in <i>σ</i>-interaction FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedron, alleviating the voltage hysteresis during discharge process. 3) Stabilizing TMO<sub>6</sub> local structure. During the charge/discharge process, the weak <i>π</i>-interaction in CoO<sub>6</sub> octahedron effectively copes with sharply shrinking of Fe─O, which was demonstrated by the EXAFS results at different SOCs. Benefitting from the improvement in the three aspects above, Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Co<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> exhibits the superior rate performance (55 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C) and fast kinetics among the three samples. Furthermore, Co<sub>0.3</sub>||hard carbon full cells exhibited a high energy density of 395&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C and a high capacity retention of 75% for 100 cycles at 1 C, representing the great potential for practical applications. We hope this study contributes to fundamental understanding of electronic structure regulation mechanism and also provides valuable information for designing high performance SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202409732-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202409732-sec-0080-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202409732-sec-0090">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202409732-sec-0100">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202409732-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202409732-sec-0110-title"> Materials Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>P2-type Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Co<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, and Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> were synthesized by the solid-state sintering process. For Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Co<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, CoO, Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, LiOH·H<sub>2</sub>O, and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> in stoichiometric proportion were mixed together with grinding in an agate mortar. Then, the mixture was heated to 450&nbsp;°C within 90&nbsp;min, kept for 4&nbsp;h, and then heated to 900&nbsp;°C within 90&nbsp;min, kept for 15&nbsp;h, in a tube furnace under O<sub>2</sub> atmosphere. Then, the sample cooled down to room temperature and was kept in Ar atmosphere. Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> were obtained through the same processes as Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Co<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> by mixing the corresponding stoichiometric Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, LiOH·H<sub>2</sub>O, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, CoO, Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, LiOH·H<sub>2</sub>O, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> as raw materials, respectively.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202409732-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202409732-sec-0120-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>The slurry of 70&nbsp;wt% active materials, 20&nbsp;wt% SuperP, and 10&nbsp;wt% polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF, Sigma-Aldrich) dissolved in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (99.5%, Sigma-Aldrich) were spread onto aluminum foils to fabricate the working electrode. Then the mixture was dried at 70&nbsp;°C at vacuum overnight. The electrochemical performance of the prepared samples was tested in coin cells (CR2032) assembled by utilizing the working electrode, a sodium tablet as counter electrode, a separator (GF/D, Whatman), and a 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in a solution of ethyl carbonate and propylene carbonate (EC: PC, 1:1 in volume) with 5&nbsp;vol% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC). A neware CT-4008Tn battery test system was used to carry out galvanostatic charge–discharge measurements in the voltage range of 1.5–4.5&nbsp;V at 25&nbsp;°C. CV and EIS measurements were conducted by utilizing a Gamry electrochemical workstation. CV was tested between 1.5 and 4.5&nbsp;V at the scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. EIS was measured in a frequency range from 10<sup>5</sup> to 0.01&nbsp;Hz. GITT was measured by applying the repeated current pulses for 30&nbsp;min at a current density of 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, followed by relaxation for 2&nbsp;h. The full cell was assembled with P2-type Na<sub>0.6</sub>Li<sub>0.1</sub>Co<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> as the cathode, hard carbon as the anode, and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC: PC = 1: 1 in volume as the electrolyte, respectively. In order to supplement sufficient Na<sup>+</sup> in hard carbon before assembling full cell, the hard carbon electrode was obtained by disassembling the half cell in the glove box after half-discharging at 40&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202409732-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202409732-sec-0130-title"> Materials Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>XRD patterns were collected by using an X-ray diffractometer (Rigaku Mini Flex 600) with Cu-Kα radiation (<i>λ</i> = 1.54 Å) at 40&nbsp;kV, 15&nbsp;mA. Then, the data were refined with the GSAS II software based on the Rietveld method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0024_R_d167077066e1876" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The in-situ XRD was tested on a Bruker D8 Advance diffractometer with Cu-Kα radiation (<i>λ</i> = 1.54 Å) in the scan range of 10°−50° at the current density of 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The Beryllium windows were utilized in the in-situ cells. Hard and soft XAS characterizations were conducted at the 7-BM (QAS) and 23-ID-2 (IOS) beamlines of National Synchrotron Light Source II (NSLS-II) at Brookhaven National Laboratory. The XANES and EXAFS data were processed and normalized by utilizing Athena software packages.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0025_R_d167077066e1886" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Synchrotron. Radiat.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The morphologies of the synthesized samples were characterized by field emission transmission electron microscope (Talos F200X G2) equipped with an EDX detector for elements analysis.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202409732-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202409732-sec-0140-title"> DFT Calculations</h4>
                  
                  <p>All the calculations are performed in the framework of the density functional theory with the projector augmented plane-wave method, as implemented in the Vienna ab initio simulation package.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0026_R_d167077066e1900" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Phys. Rev. B.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The generalized gradient approximation proposed by Perdew, Burke, and Ernzerhof is selected for the exchange-correlation potential.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0027_R_d167077066e1905" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The long range van der Waals interaction is described by the DFT-D3 approach.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0028_R_d167077066e1910" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Chem. Phys.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The cut-off energy for plane wave is set to 400&nbsp;eV. In order to consider the strong electronic correlations in the localized Mn-3d, Fe-3d, and Co-3d orbitals, on-site Hubbard <i>U</i> of 4.7, 4.4, and 5.5&nbsp;eV, respectively, was added.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202409732-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202409732-bib-0029_R_d167077066e1918" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - npj Comput. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The energy criterion is set to 10<sup>−6</sup>&nbsp;eV in iterative solution of the Kohn–Sham equation. The Brillouin zone integration is performed using a 5 × 5 × 1 <i>k</i>-mesh. All the structures are relaxed until the residual forces on the atoms have declined to less than 0.03&nbsp;eV Å<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>

    window.addEventListener('load', () => {
